This report concludes a Master’s Thesis in Medicine and Technology
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Atrial fibrillation is the most common cardiac arrhythmia in clinical practice and is
mainly found in the elderly population with an increasing prevalence. The arrhyth-
mia is characterized by uncoordinated contractions of the heart muscle as a result
of chaotic electrical impulses from the sinoatrial node, which results in the atria to
fibrillate.
Electrocardiogram is used to detect and diagnose atrial fibrillation. However, due to
long monitoring of the signal from the electrocardiogram, the detection and diagnosis
of atrial fibrillation is time-consuming. Therefore, an algorithm for detection of atrial
fibrillation is of interest.
This study aims to propose an algorithm for detection of atrial fibrillation.
The algorithm distinguishes itself from the state-of-the-art methods in that it uses
tensors to analyze multi-lead electrocardiogram recordings simultaneously.
Electrocardiogram recordings are gathered from the MIT-BIH Atrial Fibrillation
Database and MIT-BIH Arrhyhtmia Database.
Discrete Wavelet Transform and
Empirical Mode Decomposition were used the decompose the tensors into subten-
sor. From these subtensors, five statistical features were extracted: Mean, standard
deviation, variance, skewness and kurtosis. Additionally, six heart rate variability
features were extracted yielding a total of 456 features.
Additionally, machine learning was used to classify heart rhythms including
atrial fibrillation rhythm.
Although many machine learning methods exist, three
different classifiers were used: Support Vector Machine, k Nearest Neighbor and
Random Forest. For each of the classifiers, different parameters and kernel types
were used. After a thorough analysis and subsequent comparison of these classifiers,
this study finds that the performance of the proposed algorithm was best with the
Random Forest classifier.
==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Resumé
Atrieflimren er den mest udbredte hjerterytmeforstyrrelse i klinisk praksis og findes
primært blandt den ældre befolkning med en stigende prævalens. Denne hjerteryt-
meforstyrrelse er karakteriseret ved ukoordinerede sammentrækninger af hjertemusklen.
Dette sker, da de elektriske impulser fra sinuskuden er kaotiske, hvilket foråsager
atrieflimren.
Elektrokardiogram bruges til at detektere og diagnosticere atrieflimren. Grundet en
lang monitorering af signalet fra elektrokardiogrammet, vil detekteringen og diag-
nosticeringen af atrieflimren være tidskrævende. En algoritme der har til formål at
detektere atrieflimren er derfor ønskelig.
Dette studie har til formål at udvikle en algoritme til detektering af atrieflim-
ren. Denne algoritme adskiller sig fra state-of-the-art metoderne ved at den bruger
tensorer til at analysere adskillige registreringer af hjerterytmen samtidig.
Elektrokardiogramoptagelserne er samlet fra MIT-BIH Atrial Fibrillation Database
og MIT-BIH Arrhythmia Database. Discrete Wavelet Transform og Empirical Mode
Decomposition blev brugt til at dele disse tensorer i undertensorer. Fem statistiske
egenskaber blev udtrukket fra disse undertensorer: Gennemsnit, standardafvigelse,
varians, skævhed og kurtosis. Derudover blev seks egenskaber udtrukket baseret på
pulsvariationen, hvilket resulterer i 456 egenskaber i alt.
Ydermere blev machine learning brugt til at klassificere hjerterytme inklusiv
atrieflimren. Selvom der er mange machine learning metoder tilgængelige blev tre
forskellige brugt: Support Vector Machine, k Nearest Neighbor og Random Forest.
For hver af disse metoder blev forskellige parametre brugt. Efter en dybdegående
analyse og efterfølgende sammenligning finder dette studie at den udviklede algoritme
præsterede bedst med Random Forest metoden.

Preface
This master’s thesis was prepared at the Department of Health Technology at the
Technical University of Denmark in fulfillment of the requirements for acquiring a
Master of Science degree in Medicine and Technology (Biomedical Engineering). The
thesis proposes an algorithm for detection of atrial fibrillation using electrocardiogram
recordings.
The project start date is 26 August 2019 and the project end date is 26
February 2020. This thesis consists of the description of the algorithm and an analysis
of the obtained results. Additionally, the MATLAB code has been attached.
Kongens Lyngby, February 26, 2020
Ugur Erman

==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Research Motivation
Atrial fibrillation (AF) is the most common cardiac arrhythmia in clinical practice
and is mainly found in the elderly population [1] with an increasing prevalence [2].
It is estimated that by 2050 AF will affect 6-12 million people in the USA and 17.9
million people in Europe by 2060 [3]. The increase in prevalence is a result of the ag-
ing population in the world, enhanced detection of AF as well as increasing incidence
[4]. The main characteristics of AF are irregular heartbeats and the symptoms may
include shortness of breath and chest pain [5]. Although AF in itself is not necessarily
life-threatening, it is often associated with deadly complications such as heart failure
and stroke [2].
In AF, the electrical impulses from the sinoatrial (SA) node are chaotic,
which results in uncoordinated muscle contractions [6]. This causes the atria to fib-
rillate. Because of the filtration of these additional impulses by the atrioventricular
(AV) node, the ventricles of the heart will contract normally. However, the rate of
these contractions will be irregular [7]. Due to the fact that the atria contractions
are disturbed, blood can accumulate, resulting in the formation of blood clots. This
is a possible explanation of why AF is associated with stroke [6]. Therefore, early
detection and diagnosis of AF are essential to provide appropriate treatment. The
common way to treat AF is with medication such as anticoagulant [8] as well as rate
control and rhythm control [9].
An electrocardiogram (ECG) is considered to be the gold standard in the
diagnosis of AF [10]. The detection and diagnosis of AF require long monitoring
of ECG, which is both costly and time-consuming. Furthermore, AF is not always
accurately detected on the ECG by many healthcare professionals [11]. Additionally,
==PAGE:22==
2
1 Introduction
in clinical settings, immediate results are sometimes demanded, which makes time-
consuming visual inspections less feasible. Therefore, an algorithm that can detect
AF using intelligent methods is of great interest for physicians and cardiologist, since
it can help them to have a more accurate and reliable diagnosis of AF.
ECGs are usually recorded as multi-lead signals and physicians take advan-
tage of all leads simultaneously when diagnosing AF. However, most of the published
studies on the detection of AF are either based on results where
1. Only one of the ECG leads has been used as input, or
2. All of the ECG leads have been used, but they have been processed separately
and combined in a later step [12].
The main drawback of this is that some of the available information from the other
leads can be lost, especially in the models where a single lead has been considered.
In recent years, a method to analyze the multiple leads simultaneously has been of
interest. With tensors, which are higher-order generalizations of vectors and matrices,
the ECG recordings with multiple leads can be analyzed simultaneously. In biomed-
ical signal processing, tensors have been used in fields such as monitoring of epilepsy
and EEG processing [13, 14]. However, in cardiac applications, the use of tensors are
still a novel technique.
Machine learning has been widely used in algorithms developed for cardiac
applications. Although there are many different machine learning methods, the most
widely used are Artificial Neural Networks (ANN), Support Vector Machine (SVM),
Decision Tree and k-Nearest Neighbor (kNN) in cardiac applications [15]. A combi-
nation of machine learning methods with one another, known as ensemble learning,
has also been used. With this combination, it is possible to achieve a higher accuracy
of the algorithm [15]. However, as already mentioned, most of the algorithms process
the ECG leads separately and combine them in a later stage.
This study aims to develop an algorithm that processes information from
multi-lead ECG recordings simultaneously. Tensor methods will be used to develop
this algorithm.
Furthermore, machine learning techniques will be applied to clas-
sify AF from non-AF rhythms. The performance of the algorithm will be tested on
==PAGE:23==

two different databases: The MIT-BIH Arrhythmia Database (MITDB) [16] and the
MIT-BIH Atrial Fibrillation Database (AFDB) [17]. These databases are available
on PhysioNet [18].

will be presented. These will include the preprocessing and feature extraction of the
data. The used databases will also be described. Additionally, the used machine

the obtained results will be presented and discussed followed by a conclusion of the
thesis, respectively.
==PAGE:24==

==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2
Clinical Background
In this chapter, the basic anatomical and physiological principles of the cardiovascular
system are explained. Furthermore, a description of the heart’s conduction system is
presented. During AF, however, this conduction system is not working normally and
these changes caused by AF will be described.
Additionally, the physiological origin and measurement of ECG recordings will be in-
troduced. Because of AF, abnormal patterns will appear on an ECG. These abnormal
patterns will also be presented.
2.1
The Cardiovascular System
The cardiovascular system is a biological system that allows the circulation of blood
and transportation of nutrients including oxygen, carbon dioxide and hormones to
and from cells in the body. The most important components of the cardiovascular
system are the heart, blood and blood vessels [19]. The heart is a four-chambered
pump consisting of two atria and two ventricles. It is made up of muscle cells posi-
tioned in the thoracic cage between the two lungs. This compartment is called the
mediastinal space and it is contained in a double-walled sac called the pericardium
[20]. The right and left sides of the heart are separated by walls known as the septa
(interatrial and interventricular septa). Heart valves permit the blood to flow in only
one direction [20].
The wall of the heart is composed of three layers: The pericardium, the
myocardium, and the endocardium. The pericardium is a thin fibrous double-walled
sac (visceral and parietal), which covers the heart and holds it in a fixed position,
thus acting as a barrier to infection and protection of the heart. The myocardium is
the muscular part of the heart forming the walls of the atria and ventricles for them
to contract. The endocardium is the inner layer of the heart wall and consists of a
==PAGE:26==
6
2 Clinical Background
three-layered membrane. The endocardium’s function, amongst others, is to cover
the heart valves and line the heart chambers [21].
The cardiovascular system is divided into two parts: The pulmonary circu-
lation and the systemic circulation [21].
In the pulmonary circulation, the right atrium receives deoxygenated blood via the
superior and inferior vena cava. This blood is pumped to the right ventricle and
further to the lungs during systole, where the gas exchange function happens which
oxygenates the blood. In the systemic circulation, the oxygenated blood is received
in the left atrium and then pumped to the left ventricle. From the left ventricle, the
blood is pumped to all tissues through the aorta during systole [21]. A graphical
representation of the cardiovascular system can be seen in Figure 2.1. To provide
continuous blood flow through the body, the atria and ventricles must pump uninter-
rupted and regularly.
Figure 2.1: Graphical representation of the cardiovascular system. The deoxygenated
blood (blue) from all tissues is collected in the right atrium via the superior and infe-
rior vena cava where it undergoes the pulmonary circulation and becomes oxygenated.
Subsequently, the oxygenated blood (red) is pumped from the left ventricle to all tis-
sues through the aorta [21].
==PAGE:27==
2.2 The Heart as a Pump
7
2.2
The Heart as a Pump
The heart muscle has unique capabilities among other muscles in that it can generate
its own action potentials. These action potentials are generated in the myocardium
as a result of the excitation of muscle fibers. The generation of action potentials sub-
sequently results in the contraction of the heart muscle, making it possible to pump
the blood from the heart to the body. This system is called the cardiac conduction
system [21].
The SA node, located in the top of the right atrium, consists of a group
of pacemaking cells that can spontaneously depolarize. The depolarization results in
the generation of action potentials, responsible for the normal cardiac rhythm [22].
After SA node excitation, the depolarization spreads through the atria causing it to
contract. Via internodal pathways, the electrical activity from the SA node reaches
the AV node (located in the bottom of the right atrium) where it is slowed down. This
mechanism is done to prevent the atria and ventricles to contract simultaneously.
Following AV node excitation, the electrical activity is spread to the bundle of His
and further to the left and right bundle branches as well as the Purkinje fibers. This
causes the depolarization to spread to the left and right ventricle, causing them to
contract [22]. The conduction happens rapidly in the Purkinje fibers, allowing all the
contractile cells in the ventricles to contract almost simultaneously. This is necessary
for the quick ejection of blood from the heart to the body [21].
Figure 2.2 is a graphical representation of the cardiac conduction system with all its
main components.
2.2.1
Cardiac Conduction during AF
AF is one of supraventricular tachyarrhythmia which is characterized by uncoordi-
nated atrial activity [23]. During AF, the electrical activity spreading from the SA
node to the AV node is overwhelmed by impulses generated in other areas of the
atria, called ectopic sites. These impulses spread in a very chaotic pattern. Because
of the unorganized signal in the atria, the contraction in the atria will not be unified.
Therefore, the atria are said to be fibrillating rather than contracting.
Although the electrical activity is chaotic during AF, only a few of them reach the
ventricles to result in a heartbeat. The AV node, which slows down the electrical
activity, acts as a filter that prevents the AF impulses from passing through to the
ventricles. The impulses that do pass through to the ventricles are irregular and can
result in an irregular heartbeat. This heartbeat can be either slow (below 60 beats
==PAGE:28==
8
2 Clinical Background
per minute) or rapid (above 100 beats per minute). Furthermore, if this is to happen
it can result in a serious reduction in the cardiac output [24].
Figure 2.2: Illustration of the cardiac conduction system with its components [25].
2.3
The Electrocardiogram (ECG)
ECG is a recording of the heart’s electrical activity. As the cardiac muscle depolarizes
(and repolarizes), an electrical potential difference occurs. ECG is thus a recording
of the potential differences by placing electrodes on the body surface [25].
ECG is used by healthcare professionals to assess the cardiac conduction system and
to see whether or not there are abnormal patterns. Although there are three main
ECG signal acquisition methods (in-person, off-person, and on-person), the on-person
method, where electrodes are placed on the body, is the most commonly used [26].
While the number of electrodes and their placements can vary, the 12-lead ECG
configuration is the standard measurement set-up [27]. In the 12-lead ECG, electrical
activity is recorded between the electrodes in specific patterns. The electrode labeling
corresponds to the electrode location on the body:
• RA: Electrode on the right arm.
• LA: Electrode on the left arm.
• RL: Electrode on the right leg.
==PAGE:29==
2.3 The Electrocardiogram (ECG)
9
• LL: Electrode on the left leg.
• Vx: Six electrodes on the chest (V1,...,V6).
An ECG lead (or channel) is defined as the potential difference between two electrodes.
The 12 ECG leads are divided into three bipolar leads (limb leads), three unipolar
leads (augmented leads) and six chest leads. The derivation of the bipolar leads and
the unipolar leads can be seen in Table 2.1. The six chest leads are derived from the
six chest electrodes. The three unipolar leads are derived by having a (+) electrode.
However, there is no single (-) electrode. Instead, the limb electrodes are averaged to
create a virtual (-) electrode.
Table 2.1: Derivation of the bipolar leads and the unipolar leads. * indicates the
combination of the limb electrodes.

Figure 2.3: Graphical illustration of an ECG recording [28].
==PAGE:30==
10
2 Clinical Background
Three deflections recording the electrical propagation represent each heart-
beat on an ECG. These are the P wave, the QRS-complex, and the T wave. The P
wave is a representation of the depolarization occurring in the atria which spreads
from the SA node to the AV node. The QRS complex and the T wave represent the
depolarization and repolarization in the ventricles, respectively.
The PR interval, which is a measurement from the beginning of the P wave to the
beginning of the QRS complex, represents the delay of the signal that takes place in
the AV node. This delay is seen as the baseline between the P wave and the QRS
complex on the ECG. The ST segment, which is measured from the end of the QRS
complex to the beginning of the T wave, is usually isoelectric and represents the time
between the end of the ventricular depolarization and the beginning of the ventricu-
lar repolarization [25]. A graphical illustration of an ECG with the above-mentioned
deflections and intervals can be seen in Figure 2.3.
2.3.1
AF patterns on an ECG
During AF, abnormal patterns occur when an ECG is recorded. The abnormalities
include [29]:
• Absence of P wave: The atria fibrillate rather than contract during AF
because of the electrical chaos in the atria. This results in an absence of the P
wave on the ECG.
• Irregularly irregular RR-intervals (RRi): Since some of the uncoordinated
electrical impulses reach the ventricles, they can affect the heartbeat. These
impulses will behave in an irregularly irregular manner. This can be seen as
irregularly irregular RRi on the ECG where the RRi vary.
• Fibrillatory waves: Because of the chaotic electrical impulses, the isoelectric
line on the ECG is normally replaced by fibrillatory waves, also called f-waves.
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 3
State-of-the-Art
In this chapter, the most recent trends in algorithms for the detection of AF will be
described. The state-of-the-art review will include the algorithms with and without
the use of tensor methods to detect AF. Additionally, the articles presented here have
all used some of the databases that will be used in this thesis (MITDB or AFDB).
This way, it will be easier to compare the articles.
Because tensor methods are still a novel concept in the detection of AF and cardiac
applications in general, this section will include conducted studies in recent years.
The description of the articles will include the preprocessing methods, the features
that have been extracted and the different classifiers that have been used. Addition-
ally, the accuracy, specificity, sensitivity and F1-score (described in Chapter 4) of the
algorithms will be presented. Table 3.1 presents an overview of these articles.
Geirnaert et al. [7] investigated the detection of AF using tensor-based ECG
signal processing. The authors presented both matrix-based and tensor-based meth-
ods for the detection of AF in both single lead and multi-lead ECG. For the single
model, the recordings were compressed into one heartbeat using the singular value
decomposition (SVD). Multilinear singular value decomposition (MLSVD) was used
for the same purpose for the multi-lead model.
The Pan-Tompkins method [30] was used to detect R peaks. For the multi-lead model,
this was done lead by lead. Additionally, the morphological features of the ECG sig-
nal were both combined with traditional heart rate variability (HRV) features as well
as investigated individually.
An SVM classifier was used with ten-fold cross-validation. For the single lead model,
the highest F1-score (0.770) was achieved when the morphological features were com-
bined with HRV features. The lowest F1-score (0.593) was achieved when only the
morphological features were used.
For the multi-lead model, the highest F1-score (1.000) was achieved when the mor-
phological features were combined with HRV features. The lowest F1-score (0.933)
==PAGE:32==
12
3 State-of-the-Art
was achieved when only the morphological features were used. The perfect classifica-
tion was thus achieved with the multi-lead model and the two classes were perfectly
separated.
Sepulveda-Suescun et al. [31] proposed a method for the detection of AF
through HRV using a machine learning approach and Poincaré plot features. The
algorithm only treats two types of rhythms: AF rhythms and normal sinus rhythms
(NSR). The idea behind the methodology is that short events of AF should be dif-
ferentiated from NSR. Two experiments were proposed. In the first experiment, only
signals that had minimum one episode of AF or NSR were used. In the second exper-
iment, only signals from AF patients during NSR with spontaneous episodes of AF
were used.
The features used were the RRi and HRV features. The R peaks were detected using
the Pan-Tompkins method, and they were subsequently used to calculate the HRV
features. Furthermore, the authors used a Poincaré plot of the NSR and AF rhythms
to characterize both rhythms. The idea was that for NSR, the Poincaré plot would
show a cluster of points linearly. For AF rhythms, however, the points would be more
spread.
An SVM was used as a classifier for both experiments. For experiment 1, the accu-
racy and F1-score was 97.8% and 97.9%, respectively. The obtained sensitivity and
specificity were 97.9% and 97.8%, respectively. For experiment 2, the accuracy and
F1-score was 92.9% and 92.3%, respectively. The obtained sensitivity and specificity
were 91.3% and 94.3%, respectively.
Asgari et al. [32] developed an algorithm for the detection of AF without
the need of the P wave and R peak. In the preprocessing, the ECG signal was seg-
mented by dividing every T seconds into segments of length T. To remove noise and
baseline wander, band-pass and pass-band filters were used. Additionally, denoising
was achieved by a wavelet transform.
Unlike other algorithms, this algorithm does not require the detection of P waves and
R peaks. Instead, peak-to-average power and log-energy entropy were extracted as
features. The idea with the peak-to-average power method is that atrial activity usu-
ally appears at a specific frequency range. Therefore, it would be expected that the
power concentration in this range would be higher. With the log-energy entropy, the
authors could measure the disorder or randomness of the signal during AF. Because
the electrical impulses are chaotic and irregular during AF, it would be expected that
==PAGE:33==
3 State-of-the-Art
13
the entropy would be higher during AF.
An SVM was used as a classifier to segment AF or non-AF rhythms. Furthermore, a
Receiver Operating Characteristic (ROC) curve analysis was performed. The classifier
was also compared to other classifiers which were Naïve Bayesian, Linear Discrimi-
nant Analysis (LDA) and Logistic Regression. Among the different classifiers, the
Naïve Bayesian performed worst whereas the SVM classifier performed best with an
accuracy of 99.5%.
Xia et al. [33] proposed a model for detection of AF using a deep learning
approach.
Firstly, a band-pass filter and stationary wavelet transform was used to eliminate
baseline wander, noise and to transform 1D ECG data to 2D data.
Additionally,
a convolutional neural network approach was used to detect AF rhythms. The idea
with this method was that features such as RRi, P wave, and HRV features, etc. were
not necessary. The model obtained a sensitivity of 98.79%, a specificity of 97.87%
and an accuracy of 98.63%.
Lee et al. [34] developed an app for a smartphone with an algorithm for the
detection of AF. The feature that was used was the RRi.
Unlike the other proposed methods, the authors used the root mean square of the
successive differences of RRi (RMSSD), Shannon entropy (ShE) and Sample Entropy
(SampE) to classify AF. The RMSSD was normalized by the mean value of the RRi
time series. ShE was used to obtaining a quantitative measure of the signal random-
ness, which was expected to be higher with AF rhythms. SampE was used to measure
the similarity of the RRi time series. With AF rhythms, this similarity is expected
to be lower, thus giving a higher sample entropy (negative natural logarithm). The
authors used a threshold for the three methods. These thresholds were found using a
ROC curve for each method. The algorithm obtained the highest accuracy (96.14%)
with the SampE.
Dash et al. [35] proposed a model for real-time detection of AF using sta-
tistical features such as RMSSD normalized by the mean value of the RRi, ShE and
Turning Point Ratio (TPR). Additionally, the authors used heartbeat intervals in
their model to filter ectopic beats. They used RR[i]/RR[i-1] for each RRi to find
ectopic beats. The reasoning for this was that ectopic beats could potentially cause
false detection of AF. TPR was used to assess whether the RRi time series were ran-
==PAGE:34==
14
3 State-of-the-Art
dom. Furthermore, thresholds for the three methods were used. A ROC curve was
used to find these thresholds to classify AF from non-AF rhythms. The model was
tested on two databases and the highest accuracy of finding AF episodes was 99.1%
Billeci et al. [36] proposed a model for the detection of AF with ECG signal
acquired through a smartphone. The authors extracted a total of 55 features from
the RRi, P wave (absence of P wave) and Frequency Spectrum Analysis (FSA). An
ANOVA was used to determine which of these features were the most discriminant.
The authors aimed to classify the following rhythms: AF, normal rhythms, other
types of rhythms and noisy rhythms and the F1-score was used to measure the per-
formance of the algorithm.
A Least Square SVM was used as a classifier and the overall obtained F1-score was
0.98. Furthermore, it was found that the features extracted from the RRi were the
most discriminant.
Kennedy et al. [37] developed a model for the detection of AF. The study
aimed to determine if the automated detection of AF could be improved by using
Random Forest (RF) and kNN as classifiers. The performance of these classifiers
was compared to the coefficient of variance (CV), RMSSD, the coefficient of sample
entropy (CoSEn), and the median absolute deviation (MAD). All of the classifiers used
RRi features as input. The performance of the algorithm was assessed by calculating
the sensitivity, specificity, and positive predictive value (PPV). It was found that
the RF and kNN methods had the highest PPV (92.1% and 78.5%, respectively)
compared to the other methods. Additionally, the specificity of both methods was also
the highest (98.3% and 95.1%, respectively). However, the kNN had a considerably
reduced sensitivity (68.0%) compared to the other methods.
3.0.1
Summary of the State-of-the-Art Methods
The reason for the choice of these particular articles in the literature review is to show
how the authors have used different methods to detect AF. Although RRi features
and P wave absence features can be used, most of the authors use RRi features for
their algorithm. The reason for this might be that these features are easier to compute
and they provide important information for the algorithms [7].
The problem with most of these algorithms is that they only use a single lead as
input to their model, although the datasets that they use for their models typically
are recorded with multiple leads. Prior to the development of an algorithm with a
==PAGE:35==
3 State-of-the-Art
15
single lead ECG as input, it must be considered which lead should be used.
To cope with this problem, the algorithm developed in this thesis will use two-lead
ECG recordings as input for the model.
Tensors will be used to use these leads
simultaneously. Furthermore, this algorithm will differ from the listed articles in Table
3.1 as it will use different methods for decomposition of the ECG signals followed by
the extraction of statistical features of these decompositions. More about the methods
used for the algorithm is explained in the next chapter.

CHAPTER 4
Materials and
Methods
The developed algorithm in this thesis can be considered to consist of several steps.
Overall, these four steps are included in the algorithm: (1) Preprocessing of ECG
signal, (2) segmentation of the heartbeats, (3) construction of tensor and feature
extraction, and (4) classification. These four steps, the used databases as well as the
evaluation of the classification performance are described in detail in this chapter.
The algorithm designed in this thesis has been developed using MATLAB version
R2019b. A flowchart of this algorithm can be seen in Figure 4.7.
4.1
Data
4.1.1
MIT-BIH Arrhythmia Database
The MITDB is a database containing 48 half-hour ECG recordings with two channel-
s/leads from 47 subjects (two recordings are from the same patient). The recordings
were conducted at Boston’s Beth Israel Hospital. The recordings were split into a
100 series and a 200 series. Out of the 48 recordings, 23 of them were selected ran-
domly from a set of 4000 24-hour ECG recordings collected from a mixed population.
These 23 recordings are intended to represent normal clinical recordings and they
are denoted the 100 series in this database. The next 25 recordings (the 200 series)
were collected from the same set, but they are intended to represent uncommon but
clinically significant arrhythmias. The subjects included 25 men (aged 32 to 89 years)
and 22 women (aged 23 to 89 years) [16].
The recordings were digitized at 360 samples per second per channel. Each record
and each beat were annotated by two or more cardiologists independently to include
approximately 110.000 annotations in total. Furthermore, rhythm annotations have
==PAGE:38==
18
4 Materials and Methods
also been included in the annotation files.
To cope with the large memory requirements of the algorithm, the record-
ings of this database have been resampled to 125 Hz. This means that the ECG
recordings from the subjects will be described by fewer samples (approximately 1/3
of the original samples). The fewer samples result in a much less memory requirement
(also approximately 1/3).
For the MITDB dataset, a total of two classes are considered: NSR rhythm as well
as AF rhythm.
4.1.2
MIT-BIH Atrial Fibrillation Database
The AFDB is a database containing 25 two-channel ECG recordings which are 10
hours in duration. The recordings were selected from a set of 8000 24-hour ECG
recordings collected at Boston’s Beth Israel Hospital in which episodes of AF had
been diagnosed. Both ECG signals were digitized at 250 samples per second. An-
notation files that include annotations of the rhythm had been prepared manually.
They have been revised to be consistent with the annotations in the MITDB [17].
This dataset has been resampled to half of the original sampling frequency.
The new sampling frequency is thus 125 Hz. This has also been done to cope with the
large memory requirements. Due to the long recordings, the memory requirements
are large. Therefore, this dataset has been split into 6 groups, where the first 5 groups
contain recordings from 4 subjects. The last group contains recordings of 3 subjects.
This has only been done for the steps after the tensor construction.
4.2
Preprocessing of ECG Signal
During the recording of ECG signals, it is very common that artifacts can occur.
Several different artifacts can be present and for different reasons. Artifacts include
powerline interference, electromyogram (EMG) noise, baseline wander and motion ar-
tifacts [38]. The powerline artifacts are mainly caused by electromagnetic fields from
nearby machines. EMG noise is primarily caused by the activity of the heart muscle.
Baseline wander is noise with low frequency and is typically caused by respiration and
movement of the body. Finally, the motion artifacts are caused by the movement of
the electrodes on the patient’s skin causing a change in the electrode-skin impedance
==PAGE:39==
4.2 Preprocessing of ECG Signal
19
[38].
The baseline wander can be seen on an ECG as a baseline value that is
fluctuating (wandering). It typically has a frequency of 0.15 - 0.50 Hz and can cause
problems in the detection of the different peaks in an ECG signal [38, 39]. To re-
move baseline wander from the signal, Discrete Wavelet Transform (DWT) has been
applied.
The idea with DWT is that the input signal is passed through a series of
low-pass and high-pass filters [40]. This results in a decomposition of the signal into
different frequency scales. The high-pass filters filter out the low frequencies of the
signal and output the high frequencies. The low-pass filters do the opposite. The
low-frequency components are denoted the approximation coefficients and the high-
frequency components are denoted the detail coefficient. To decompose the signal,
wavelets (also called mother wavelets) are used. There are several types of wavelets
including Haar, Daubechies, Symlet, etc. It has been shown that the Daubechies
wavelets perform well in regards to denoising of the signals [40]. The wavelet used
for the DWT in the preprocessing step is the Daubechies 4 (db4) wavelet which
resembles the ECG signal. The signal is decomposed into 8 levels, D1 −D8 and
the low frequencies for baseline wander are set to zero. Subsequently, the inverse
process (the inverse DWT) is performed to reconstruct the denoised signal [41]. An
illustration of the principles of DWT is shown in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Illustration of the DWT. The input signal, x(n) is passed through a series
of high-pass filters, h(n), and low-pass filters, g(n). For each filtering step, the signal
is downsampled with a factor 2, meaning that there are half as many samples as
before the filtering. This is illustrated by ↓2. Di denote the detail coefficients and
Ai denote the approximation coefficients [40].
==PAGE:40==
20
4 Materials and Methods
4.3
Segmentation of Heartbeat
The segmentation of the heartbeats is based on the R peaks of the ECG signal. To
segment the heartbeat, the R peaks must, therefore, be found. The PhysioNet WFDB
Toolbox includes a function named ecgpuwave. This function takes in an ECG record
and locates the QRS complexes. Additionally, it outputs the beginning, peak, and
end of the P wave, QRS complex and T wave. The function is based on the Pan
Tompkins algorithm [18].
The segmentation is conducted with a 50% overlap. Different segment lengths are
used in the algorithm. Segment lengths of M = {20, 60, 128} beats have been inves-
tigated. Furthermore, the different segments have been labeled using the majority
vote. This means that if most of the segment contains AF rhythm, then the whole
segment will be labeled AF.
For each of these segments, feature scaling has been conducted to normalize
the range of the features of the data. The normalization method used is the zero
mean unit variance normalization, also known as the z-score normalization which is
defined as
x′ = x −ˆx
σ
(4.1)
where x is the original feature vector, ˆx is the mean of the feature vector and σ is the
standard deviation of the feature vector [42].
4.4
Tensor Construction and Feature Extraction
Tensors, the higher-order generalizations of vectors and matrices, are used to analyze
the multiple leads used in the ECG recordings simultaneously.
The order of the
tensor denotes the dimension of the array. The filtered and segmented signals have
been tensorized. A graphical illustration of a third-order tensor can be seen in Figure
4.2.
Although the segments have the same number of beats, they might differ in the
number of samples that describe them. Therefore, the longest segment (in samples)
is found and all the other segments are zero-padded to match the longest segment.
This is conducted for all the leads. Subsequently, the segmented signals from all the
leads are stacked in a tensor. The 3rd-order tensor can be denoted Rn1×n2×n3 where
n1 is the number of segments, n2 is the length of each segment in samples and n3
is the number of leads/channel. For the MITDB and AFDB databases, n3 will thus
==PAGE:41==
4.4 Tensor Construction and Feature Extraction
21
be equal to two. The features that are used in the algorithm are extracted from this
tensor.
Figure 4.2: Graphical illustration of (a) a vector, (b) a matrix and (c) a third-order
tensor [26].
4.4.1
Discrete Wavelet Transform and Empirical Mode
Decomposition
The DWT, which is described in section 4.2, is performed on the constructed tensor
resulting in subtensors. The same approach as in the preprocessing step has been
used on the tensor, except the reconstruction part. This means that the decomposi-
tion level is 8 using the Daubechies 4 wavelet. The decompositions result in detail
coefficients D1−D8 and one approximation coefficient, giving a total of 9 subtensors.
The decomposition resulted in a 4th-order tensor Rn1×n2×n3×n4, where n1 is the num-
ber of segments, n2 is the number of subtensors, n3 is the number of samples in each
segment and n4 is the number of channels. The subtensors are further decomposed
using Empirical Mode Decomposition (EMD).
EMD is an algorithm to decompose non-stationary time series into compo-
nents named Intrinsic Mode Functions (IMFs). The idea with the algorithm is that
any time series consists of simple intrinsic mode oscillations. IMFs are oscillatory
functions that have frequencies varying in time. They can represent characteristics
of non-stationary time series locally and their sum is equal to the original signal.
There are two conditions that IMFs must satisfy [43]:
• The number of local minima and maxima differs at most by one.
• The mean value of an IMF is zero.
==PAGE:42==
22
4 Materials and Methods
The IMFs are found through a method called sifting. The sifting method
includes the following steps [44]:
1. Connect the local maxima with a spline. This will cover the upper envelope of
the signal and be denoted A.
2. Connect the local minima with a spline. This will cover the lower envelope of
the signal and be denoted B.
3. The mean envelope (m = (A + B)/2) is subtracted from the original signal.
4. Step 1, 2 and 3 are repeated until an IMF is obtained. The criteria for this is
that the two conditions above should be satisfied.
5. When an IMF is obtained, it is subtracted from the original signal. The out-
put/residual is considered as new data and step 1 is repeated with this output.
6. All the steps are repeated until the residual is monotone and the algorithm is
completed.
In summary, the EMD algorithm can be described as
x(k) =
N
∑
i=1
ci(k) + r(k)
(4.2)
where ci(k), i = 1, ..., N is the number of IMFs and r(k) is the residual. The number
of IMFs chosen in the AF detection algorithm is 5. This means that although the
EMD algorithm finds for instance 10 IMFs for each subtensor, IMFs 6-10 were dis-
carded. The EMD algorithm resulted in a 5th-order tensor Rn1×n2×n3×n4×n5, where
n1 is the number of segments, n2 is the number of subtensors, n3 is the number of
samples in each segment, n4 is the number of IMFs and n5 is the number of channels.
The dimensions of this tensor were further rearranged to become a 3rd-order tensor
Rn1×n2×n3, where n1 is the DWT-EMD per channel decomposition result, n2 is the
number of samples per segment and n3 is the number of segments.
The DWT-EMD per channel decomposition should be interpreted like this: With 9
levels from the DWT, 5 IMFs from the EMD algorithm and 2 channels, n1 will be 90
in total. The first part of the 90 will be (D1, IMF1, Channel 1), the second part will
be (D2, IMF1, Channel 1), etc. For all the parts, 5 statistical features were extracted:
mean, variance, standard deviation, skewness, and kurtosis yielding a total of 450
features from the decompositions. Before the feature extraction, all the tensors from
==PAGE:43==
4.5 ADASYN: Adaptive Synthetic Sampling
23
each subject in the dataset were stacked into one tensor. This was done by concate-
nating the tensors along the third dimension, n3. To do this, zero-padding had to be
conducted on the second dimension, n2, as the array dimensions must be consistent
to concatenate tensors.
4.4.2
Heart Rate Variability Features
HRV is a variation in time between adjacent heartbeats. HRV is also called RRi as
the RRi is the difference in time between two R peaks (two heartbeats’ R peaks) and
can be written as
RRi = Rn −Rn−1
fs
(4.3)
where fs is the sampling frequency [45]. Six HRV features have been extracted as it
has been shown that HRV features contain a lot of information in terms of classifying
AF rhythm [7]. The features are mean of the RRi, standard deviation of RRi, RMSSD
between adjacent RRi, normalized RMSSD (normalized by the mean RRi) between
adjacent RRi, CV of the RRi and minimal RRi. In total, 456 features have been
extracted and they are all listed in Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Description of the extracted features.
Feature
Feature Description
F1-F90
Mean of DWT+EMD decomposition
F91-F180
Standard deviation of DWT+EMD decomposition
F181-F270
Variance of DWT+EMD decomposition
F271-F360
Skewness of DWT+EMD decomposition
F361-F450
Kurtosis of DWT+EMD decomposition
F451
Mean RRi
F452
Standard deviation of RRi
F453

F454
Normalized RMSSD of adjacent RRi
F455

F456

4.5
ADASYN: Adaptive Synthetic Sampling
The MITDB is a highly imbalanced dataset. Due to this, the number of non-AF
beats is much larger than the number of AF beats. The problem with such data is
that, because of the dominance of one class, the trained model will highly under-fit.
==PAGE:44==
24
4 Materials and Methods
The reason for this is that the model will not be able to classify the minority class suc-
cessfully. The Adaptive Synthetic Sampling (ADASYN) method can overcome this
problem by generating synthetic data. This method has been applied on the MITDB.
The input of this method is a training dataset Dr with m samples {xi, yi},
i = 1, ..., m. xi is an instance in a feature space and yi is the label corresponding
to xi. ms and ml represent the number of minority class instances and the number
of majority class instances, respectively.
It can thus be said, that ms ≤ml and
ms + ml = m [46].
The procedure for generating the synthetic data is as follows:
1. The degree of imbalance is calculated: d = ms
ml where d ∈[0, 1].
2. The number of synthetic data examples needed to be generated for the minority
class is calculated: G = (ml −ms) × β. β ∈[0, 1] represents the level of balance
subsequent to the generated synthetic data. For β = 1, the dataset will be fully
balanced. For the MITDB dataset, β = 1 is used.
3. For each instance in the minority class, the k nearest neighbors are found based
on the Euclidean distance (this is explained in more details in section 4.6.3).
Subsequently, the ratio ri is calculated and it is defined as ri = ∆i
K , i = 1, ..., ms.
∆i denotes the number of samples in the k nearest neighbor of instances that
are a part of the majority class.
4. Normalize rx = ri/
ms
∑
i=1
ri. rx becomes a density distribution.
5. The amount of synthetic data needed to be generated for each minority instance
xi is calculated as gi = rx × G. Here, G represents the same as in point 2.
6. For each xi in the minority class, two steps are conducted:
a) A minority class instance, xu from the k nearest neighbor for xi is chosen
at random.
b) The synthetic data is generated by si = xi + (xu −xi) × λ. Here (xu −xi)
is a difference vector and λ ∈[0, 1] [46].
==PAGE:45==
4.6 Classification
25
4.6
Classification
This section introduces the machine learning classifiers that have been used in the
algorithm. A short description of these classifiers will be presented as well as how
they have been used in the algorithm.
4.6.1
Support Vector Machine
SVM is a popular class of supervised machine learning algorithms that can be used
for both classification and regression [47]. SVM can be used for binary classification
problems which this AF detection algorithm also deals with.
The idea with SVM is to fit a hyperplane that can separate two classes. To do so,
the margin between the two classes is maximized. The maximized margin will result
in the largest distance between the hyperplane and the closest data points from both
classes. Suppose that a dataset has N inputs and targets: Z = {(x1, t1), ..., (xn, tn)}
with input vectors xn ∈Rm and targets tn ∈R. By using this dataset, the SVM
algorithm will approximate a function f(x), which maps the inputs to targets:
f(x) ≃
N
∑
n=1
(wT xn + b)
(4.4)
where w denotes the weight vector and b denotes a bias term [48]. A definition of
the hyperplane can be achieved by the determination of both w and b. The optimal
hyperplane (wT xn + b = 0) has the margin
2
∥w∥where ∥w∥= √w · w. When all
data points are on the ”correct” side of the margin, all the points will be perfectly
classified. Otherwise, misclassifications happen.
Originally, SVM was developed for data that were linearly separable [26].
However, the algorithm was later extended to include the classification of non-linear
data. This is possible with a feature map (ϕ(·) : Rn →RN). The idea with the feature
map is that the input data are mapped into a higher-dimensional feature space, and
only the inner product of the feature maps is required.
The inner products are
replaced by kernel functions. This is called the kernel trick. The kernel function is
defined as follows:
K(x, z) = ϕ(x)T ϕ(z)
(4.5)
There are several kernels that can be used. However, mainly three different kernels
are commonly used. These are
==PAGE:46==
26
4 Materials and Methods
1. The linear kernel. K(x, z) = xT z
2. The polynomial kernel: K(x, z) = (xT z + τ)d. d denotes the polynomial degree
and τ ≥0
3. The Radial Basis Function (RBF) kernel: K(x, z) = exp
(
−∥x−x′∥2
2σ2
)
.
For this algorithm, all three kernel types have been used. Furthermore, the
polynomial degrees used are 2 and 3, yielding a quadratic kernel and cubic kernel.
4.6.2
Random Forest
RF is an ensemble learning approach that uses a large number of individual decision
trees. Each of the individual decision trees makes a prediction. Subsequently, the
most frequently occurring prediction (label) will be chosen as the model’s prediction
[49].
Decision trees, which are the building blocks of the RF method, are used to answer
a series of questions about the features. The answers are used to split the features
into subcomponents. This procedure is repeated until all the subcomponents have
the same label and no further splits can occur.
Training deep decision trees can lead to over-fitting.
To cope with this problem,
different deep decision trees are used on different parts of the training set [48]. An
illustration of the RF method is shown in Figure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Illustration of the RF method. Two different classes are considered: class
X and class Y. For n = 3, the final class will be X [50].
.
==PAGE:47==
4.6 Classification
27
4.6.3
k-Nearest Neighbor
The kNN algorithm is one of the simplest machine learning algorithms for classifica-
tion. The basic idea of the kNN algorithm is to assign a data point a label based on
the label of its nearest neighbor data points [39].
Overall, the kNN algorithm consists of three steps:
1. Calculate the distance between the data point that is to be labeled and all the
other data points in the training set.
2. Choose the k nearest data points.
3. Apply majority voting for classifying the data point. The label that occurs most
frequently in the pool is assigned to the data point.
In point 1, the distance is usually calculated using the Euclidean distance, d, which
is defined as
d =
v
u
u
t
N
∑
i=1
(xi −yi)2
(4.6)
Different values for k have been considered. In this algorithm, k = {3, 5, 7, 9} have
been used for classification. An illustration of the kNN algorithm can be seen in
Figure 4.4.
Figure 4.4: Illustration of the kNN algorithm. In the first pool, where k=3, the green
point will be classified as a red triangle. In the second pool, where k=5, the green
point will be classified as a blue square [51].

4 Materials and Methods
4.7
Performance Evaluation
The MITDB dataset and the AFDB dataset have both been used for training and
testing separately. For each dataset, all the data have been split into a training set
and a testing set. The testing set counts for approximately 80% of the whole data
and the testing set counts for approximately 20% of the whole data.
The reason for the independent analysis of these two datasets is found in
their annotation. For the MITDB datasets, NSR rhythms, AF rhythms as well as
other rhythms have been annotated. For the AFDB dataset, however, AF rhythms,
atrial flutter rhythms, AV junctional rhythms, and other rhythms have been anno-
tated. For the other rhythms group, it has not been specified which rhythms are
included. Therefore, it can not be known whether or not NSR are included in this
group.
For the MITDB dataset, the data has been split into training and testing as follows
(the numbers are subject numbers):
Training: 100, 101, 102, 106, 107, 108, 109, 111, 112, 113, 114, 115, 116, 117, 119,
121, 122, 124, 200, 201, 203, 207, 209, 213, 214, 215, 217, 220, 221, 222, 223, 228, 230,
231, 232, 233, 234.
Testing: 103, 104, 118, 123, 202, 205, 210, 212, 219.
For the AFDB dataset, the data has been split into training and testing as follows:
Training: 04015, 04043, 04048, 04126, 04746, 04908, 04936, 05091, 05121, 05261,
06426, 06453, 06995, 07162, 07859, 07879.
Testing: 07910, 08215, 08219, 08378, 08405, 08434, 08455.
For both datasets, a 10-fold cross-validation scheme has been used on the
training set. This means that the training set is further divided into 10 parts, where
the training is conducted on 90% of the data and validation is conducted on 10% of
==PAGE:49==
4.7 Performance Evaluation
29
the data. This is done 10-times, meaning that the total size of the validation part
will be equal to the size of the input data. The idea with the cross-validation is that
the model does not know the 10% of the data (validation set), which it is testing its
performance on. An illustration of a 10-fold cross-validation is shown in Figure 4.5.
All the trained models with the different classifiers and their different parameters will
be used on the test sets.
Furthermore, the training on the MITDB dataset has been conducted only
after ADASYN has been applied.
The reason for this is that the number of AF
rhythm in this dataset is too insufficient to classify AF on new data. The models will
not see enough AF instances during the training phase when ADASYN has not been
applied.
Besides the independent training and testing on the two different datasets,
a third experiment has been conducted. In this experiment, the trained models from
the AFDB dataset are used on the whole MITDB dataset. Thus, training and valida-
tion have been conducted on one dataset (AFDB) and the testing has been conducted
on another dataset (MITDB). However, only AF features and thus segments that are
labeled as AF rhythm will be used as input for the trained model. For the testing
set, all 48 subjects are included and no ADASYN has been applied.
Confusion matrices, as well as ROC curve plots, are used to assess the
performance of the models. A description of these follows in the next sections.
==PAGE:50==
30
4 Materials and Methods
Figure 4.5: 10-fold cross-validation. The input data is split into a training part (white
boxes) and a validation part (grey boxes). 90% of the data is assigned to the training
part and the last 10% is assigned to the validation part. This is conducted for 10
iterations [52].
4.7.1
Confusion matrix
A confusion matrix is used to evaluate the performance of a machine learning classifica-
tion problem. It can be used for binary classification as well as classification problems
involving more than 2 classes. A confusion matrix is a table with 4 different combi-
nations of predicted classes and true classes. It is used to calculate sensitivity (also
known as recall), specificity, PPV (also known as precision) as well as the accuracy.
The 4 different combinations are True Positive (TP), True Negative (TN),
False Positive (FP) and False Negative (FN). TP denotes the instances where the
model correctly predicts the positive class, which in this case is the AF rhythm
instances. TN denotes the instances where the model correctly predicts the negative
class, which in this case is the non-AF rhythm instances. FP denotes the instance
where the model predicts the positive class but where the actual class is the negative
class. Finally, FN denotes the instances where the model predicts the negative class,
but where the actual class is the positive class. FP is also known as Type I error and
==PAGE:51==
4.7 Performance Evaluation
31
FN is also known as Type II error. Figure 4.6 shows an illustration of a confusion
matrix.
Figure 4.6: Illustration of a confusion matrix. The green boxes highlight the true
predictions and the red boxes highlight the false predictions.
With the 4 different combinations, the following metrics can be calculated:
Accuracy =
TP + TN
TP + TN + FP + FN
(4.7)
Sensitivity =
TP
TP + FN
(4.8)
Specificity =
TN
TN + FP
(4.9)
PPV =
TP
TP + FP
(4.10)
where sensitivity is a measure of the proportion of the actual positive class
that was correctly predicted. Specificity, on the other hand, measures the proportion
of the actual negative class that was correctly predicted. PPV measures the propor-
tion of the predicted positive class that was TP. Finally, accuracy is the fraction of
predictions that the model made correctly.
==PAGE:52==
32
4 Materials and Methods
For imbalanced datasets, however, the accuracy is often replaced by the
so-called F1-score, which is defined as
F1 = 2. Precision.Recall
Precision + Recall
(4.11)
The F1-score is the harmonic mean of sensitivity and PPV and has a value between
0 and 1, where 1 shows the highest classification ability [48].
4.7.2
Receiver Operating Characteristics Curve
The ROC curve is a graphical plot which is used to assess the diagnostic ability of
a classifier. In an ROC curve, the true positive rate (sensitivity or recall) is plotted
against the false positive rate (1-specificity) at various threshold settings [26]. By
plotting the ROC curve for the different models in the same plot, it is easy to com-
pare the different models and find the optimal model for the particular classification
problem.
Additionally, a well-chosen decision threshold can be found as different
thresholds give different predictions and thus a different performance of the model.
A way of quantifying the ROC curve is through the calculation of the Area
Under the Curve (AUC), which (as the name states) calculates the area under the
ROC curve. The value of AUC is between 0 and 1, where 1 represents a model with
perfect classification, thus a perfect separation of the classes. Additionally, for AUC
= 1, the optimal decision threshold is at point (0, 1) on the ROC curve as this point
represents the highest sensitivity and the highest specificity possible.
Figure 4.7: Flowchart of the proposed algorithm.

5.1
Filtering of Signal
The aim of the filtering with DWT is to remove noise and baseline wander. Figure
5.1 shows an image of the result of this filtering. The figure shows ECG recordings
from both leads from subject 234 in the MITDB dataset. The two images on the left
represent recordings before filtering and the two images on the right represent record-
ings after DWT filtering. The images show the first 7200 samples only. Evidently,
the DWT filtering has been able to successfully remove the baseline wander. Further-
more, the filtering has not resulted in a deformation of the signals or smoothing of
the QRS complexes which could influence the location of the R peak.
Figure 5.1: Illustration of the first 7200 samples of ECG recording from the two leads
before and after filtering. Subject 234 is from the MITDB dataset.

5.2
Balancing the Dataset
As the MITDB dataset is highly imbalanced, the minority class is oversampled with
ADASYN such that the number of labels in the minority class will be almost equal
to the number of labels in the majority class.
Table 5.1 shows the number of segments before and after ADASYN for the
majority class and the minority class. The numbers should be interpreted as follows:

A before ADASYN part (MITDB), an after ADASYN part (MITDB) and the number
of segments in the AFDB dataset where no ADASYN has been applied. Furthermore,
the table shows the values for all the segment lengths with the majority vote.

The increase in AF segments is further highlighted by the plots in Figure
5.2. The two figures show the standard deviation of RRi against the mean RRi before
ADASYN (Figure 5.2a) and after ADASYN (Figure 5.2b). The scatter plots show
the features for the MITDB dataset only with M = 128beats per segment. NSR seg-
ments are marked with blue circles and label 0, and AF segments are marked with red
circles and label 1. In Figure 5.2b, it is evident that the population of AF segments
are much denser after ADASYN than before ADASYN, which is consistent with the
numbers in Table 5.1. These numbers show the increase in AF segments after the
application of ADASYN.
The normal range for RRi is approximately 0.6 −1.2 seconds [53]. As seen
in the two plots in Figure 5.2 most of the RRi lie in this range for both classes.
Furthermore, as most of the beats on an ECG will be classified as normal, it will be
expected that most of them will be similar in terms of morphology as well as the RRi.
Therefore, a low standard deviation of the heartbeats is expected. When looking at
==PAGE:55==
5.2 Balancing the Dataset
35
the plots, it is evident that the standard deviation of the NSR segments is very close
to zero. This is consistent with the findings in Peimankar et al. [48]. Furthermore, it
can be seen that although most of the blue circles are located on the left side of the
plots (low standard deviation), circles closer to the middle and on the right side of
the plots do occur. The reason for this could be the application of the majority vote
on the segments. For instance, if 70 beats contain NSR rhythm and 58 beats contain
AF rhythm in a 128 beats segment, the whole segment will be labeled as an NSR
segment. However, since several of these beats in the segment contain AF rhythm,
this will influence the features, because the features represent a mix of both rhythm
types, but the whole segment is labeled as NSR.

were zero-padded to match the length of the longest segment. An illustration of this
can be seen in Figure 5.3b. Here, it is quite evident that after the last heartbeat, the
amplitude of the signals is zero for approximately the last 80 samples as a result of
the zero-padding.

5.4
Classification of Heart Rhythm
This section deals with the classifiers that have been used and their performance in
different phases. The section is broadly split into three parts: The first part deals
==PAGE:57==
5.4 Classification of Heart Rhythm
37
with AF detection in the AFDB database. The second part deals with AF detection
in the MITDB database. The third part deals with using the trained models from the
AFDB database and testing them on the MITDB database. For the results that are
reported for both databases, they have been calculated the following way: For each
of the folds in the 10-fold cross-validation, a confusion matrix is generated, showing
the TP, TN, FP, and FN. These confusion matrices are summed such that the final
confusion matrix contains the results from the confusion matrices of all the folds.
This has been done both for the training part and the validation part. Because 10%
of the data is used for validation 10 times, the sum of the TP, TN, FP, and FN will be
equal to the size of the input data that is fed to the classifiers trained. The accuracy,
sensitivity, specificity, PPV and F1-score have been calculated based on the summed
confusion matrices based on the equations given in section 4.7.1. Furthermore, tables
and bar plots will be used to show the result of these metrics. For the training and
validation part, only results from the validation part are shown, because the validation
part contains unseen data for the models trained. Thus the validation part gives a
better indication of how the model performs when unseen data is presented.
5.4.1
AFDB Database
The results of the training and validation part in this database are different from the
ones in the MITDB database. For unknown reasons, the MATLAB program was not
able to train models with the SVM and the kNN classifiers for the M = 20 beats
segments and M = 128 beats segments. This counts for all the different kernels and
parameters used. Despite multiple tries, no models were successfully trained. For
these segment lengths, it was possible to train and validate models with the RF clas-
sifiers and all the parameters used. For the segment length of M = 60 beats, it was
possible to train models with all the three classifiers and with different parameters
and kernels.

From Table 5.2, it is evident that in the validation set, increasing the number
of trees in the RF algorithm increases both the accuracy of the model as well as the
F1-score. Thus, the RF model with 50 trees has the highest accuracy of 95.79% and
F1-score of 0.9532. The trade-off, however, is a slight decrease in the sensitivity of
the model. Although it is not by much, the RF model with 20 trees seems, however,
to perform best when it comes to detecting AF instances followed by 40 trees and 50
trees. For these three models, the AUC is 0.98 and almost a perfect separation of the
two classes has therefore been obtained.
Another trade-off of increasing the number of trees is the computational
time. As seen in Table 5.2, the training time is proportional to the number of trees.
Although not by much, the model with 50 trees, therefore, takes the longest time to
complete. The time between the slowest and fastest model is still below 2 minutes,
which is not a huge difference.
==PAGE:59==
5.4 Classification of Heart Rhythm
39
For the testing set (Table 5.3), however, the results are different. Here, an
increase in the number of trees does not necessarily result in higher accuracy. Besides
the model with 5 trees, there is no proportionality when it comes to the accuracy and
number of trees. The model with 5 trees performs best in all metrics besides the
specificity followed by the model with 50 trees.
For the 5 trees case, with a sen-
sitivity of 82.86%, the model distinguishes itself from the other parameters, and it
also performs best when it comes to correctly finding the instances of AF. With the
highest AUC value, this model is also best able to distinguish between the two classes.
Figure 5.4 shows a confusion matrix (Figure 5.4a) and a ROC curve plot
(Figure 5.4b) for the 5 tree model with RF classifier and with M = 20 beats per
segment. Although the model has been able to find a good amount of TPs and TNs,
the number of FPs and FNs are quite high as well, which in turn decreases the overall
accuracy of the model. Finally, Figure 5.5 shows the confusion matrix for the 50 trees
model on the validation set and with the same segment length.

In Table 5.4, it can be seen that the proportionality between the number
of trees and the accuracy and F1-score is still present. The model with 50 trees has
the highest accuracy and F1-score of 96.87% and 0.9651, respectively. Contrary to
the M = 20 beats segment, with 50 trees the sensitivity of 97.69% is also the highest
among the number of trees. The specificity is only slightly behind that of the model
with 40 trees (96.22% against 96.24%).
Another thing that can be noticed is the training time of the models. With
the M = 60 beats segment, it is still true that for a higher number of trees, the train-
ing time increases. The 50 trees model takes almost double the amount of time that
the 5 trees model takes to compute. However, the difference is only approximately 1
minute for models that are already quickly trained.
The results for the test set in Table 5.5 are slightly different. Again, all
the metrics results are lower in this set compared to the validation set. However,
contrary to the M = 20 beats segment results, the model with 20 trees performs best.
Except for the sensitivity, specificity, and AUC, this model has the highest accuracy
and F1-score of 76.87% and 0.7582, respectively. For the model with 5 trees, it is
noticeable that the model is good at predicting AF instances with a sensitivity of
89.14%. However, for non-AF instances, the model does not perform well and has a
specificity of 58.48%.

In Table 5.6, it is evident that yet again, the model with the most number
of trees outperforms all the other models. The validation accuracy and F1-score are
the highest at 96.38% and 0.9598, respectively. With an AUC of 0.99, the model has
obtained an almost perfect separation of the classes during the validation phase. As
with the results with the shorter segment lengths, the trade-off of the high accuracy
and F1-score is the training time. Increasing the number of trees seems to increase
the training time throughout the segment lengths.
When looking at the training time in general for the different segment
lengths (Table 5.2, Table 5.4 and Table 5.6), it is noticeable that shortening the
segments results in a longer training time. This makes sense when taking into consid-
eration how many points are analyzed during the training phase. When shortening
the segments, each segment will be shorter. However, on the other hand, the number
of segments will increase. The input for the different classifiers is a feature matrix of
==PAGE:63==
5.4 Classification of Heart Rhythm
43
size AxB where A is the number of segments and B is the number of features and
labels. With 456 features and 1 column vector with labels, B will thus be 457. If the
feature matrix for the longest segment is, for instance, 12000x457 and it is 25000x457
for the M = 60 beats segment, the classifiers will analyze approximately double the
number of points when the segment length is approximately halved.
For the test set, the results are far from the validation set and the test
sets of the shorter segments. The best performing model is the one with 10 trees
with an accuracy of 64.70% and F1-score of 0.6244.
When inspecting the results
through the different segment length, it seems that there is some upper limit where
the models begin to have difficulty in distinguishing between the two classes. This is
somehow consistent with the findings in [35]. In this study, however, it was the other
way around. Their model performed better for an increased segment length. Their
findings were that for segment length of below M = 64, their model was not robust
enough and had difficulty distinguishing between classes.

longest segments, only validation results and test results for M = 60 beats segments
will be shown. Table 5.8 and Table 5.9 show the validation set results and test set

As seen in Table 5.8, for all the kernel types, the achieved accuracy is very
high. The SVM with a quadratic kernel achieves the highest value across all the met-
rics except specificity. With an accuracy, sensitivity, and AUC of 98.02%, 98.43%
and 0.99, respectively, not only is it very good at finding AF instances, but it almost
perfectly separates the two classes. Another advantage of this kernel type is the train-
ing time. For all the kernel types, the quadratic kernel requires the least time to train
a model.
In general, when comparing the SVM classifier and RF classifier with M =
60 beats per segment, the SVM classifier takes a lot more time to train a model.
The SVM classifier with a linear kernel, which takes the shortest time to train, takes
approximately 3 times longer time to train when compared to the RF classifier with
50 trees (the slowest to train). Additionally, the SVM classifier outperforms the RF
classifier when it comes to the validation set.
For the test set, however, the results are different. Here, for all the kernel
types, the metrics are lower in general when compared to the RF classifier. The inter-
esting observation with the SVM classifier is that it is good at finding NSR instances
(not with the linear kernel), as the specificity is quite high.
For the RBF kernel,
the specificity is 95.94% followed by 87.60% with the quadratic kernel. Again, the
quadratic kernel has the highest accuracy as well as AUC, but the highest F1-score
has been achieved with the linear kernel. This kernel also has the highest sensitivity
(recall) as well as F1-score. As the F1-score is the harmonic mean of the recall and
precision, this is probably the reason for the highest achieved F1-score.

the predictions for the two classes (other rhythms (0) and AF rhythm (1)) with the

plot, the blue dots are correctly predicted other rhythms and the orange dots are
correctly predicted AF rhythm. The incorrect predictions are marked with crosses.

In Table 5.10, it is evident that for decreasing the value of k, all the metrics
(except the AUC) increase. Thus, the best performing model is the one with k = 3
neighbors. With an accuracy, sensitivity and F1-score of 97.75%, 97.61% and 0.9746,
the model with 3 neighbors performs best overall and it also outperforms the other
models when it comes to correctly find AF instances. The only metric where it lacks
behind the other models is the AUC. However, the difference is very small. For k =
5, 7 and 9 the AUC of 1.00 indicates that these models have been able to perfectly
separate the two classes.

training time. Although with k = 5 neighbors it takes the shortest time to train, it
still almost takes 18 minutes to complete. This is slower than all the other classifiers
used except the SVM classifier with an RBF kernel. In general, though, the kNN
classifier takes a longer time to train than the other classifiers.

==PAGE:67==

is a result of the models not being able to find NSR instances properly. With an

F1-score, the difference across the models is minimal. Therefore, the difference be-

and no parameter seems to distinguish itself from others. With accuracies, F1-scores
and AUCs of approximately 50%, 0.58 and 0.55, respectively, this could mean that
the models make random guesses when it comes to labeling the data as either NSR
rhythm or AF rhythm.
5.4.2
MITDB Database
Unlike the AFDB database, it was possible to successfully train models with all the
classifier types and their different parameters and kernel types. However, only the
results for the validation set and test set for M = 20 and M = 60 beats segment
will be shown. For M = 128 beats, the results are listed in Appendix A. Again, the
results will be split into subsections according to the classifiers used, starting with
the RF classifier followed by the SVM and kNN classifiers, respectively.
5.4.2.1
RF With Different Number of Trees
In Table 5.12 and Table 5.13, the results for the RF classifier with different number
of trees and M = 60 beats segment are shown. The results are for the validation set
and test set, respectively.
For the validation set (Table 5.12), it is evident that for the 30 trees model,
40 trees model, and 50 trees model, the results are the same. Although they are very
close to the other models, these models outperform the other models in pretty much
all the metrics. Only the model with 20 trees has the same sensitivity and specificity
as the best models. For the best models, the accuracy, sensitivity, specificity, and
F1-score are 99.18%, 99.57% and 98.80% and 0.9919, respectively.
As three of the models have the same results, it seems that for 30 trees
and more, a sort of plateau occurs, where beyond this number of trees, no additional
information is obtained. This is also confirmed by the common AUC of 0.99191 of
==PAGE:68==
48
5 Results and Discussion
the best models. Additionally, when looking at the models in general, it seems that
for an increasing number of trees, all the metrics increase. Another noticeable thing
is the training time.
As it was also seen for the AFDB database, increasing the
number of trees also increases the training time. Although the 50 trees model takes
approximately double the time to train compared to the 5 trees model, in total, the
difference is only approximately 2 seconds.

For the test set (Table 5.13), however, the results are quite different. Here,
the exact opposite is present, in which for a decreasing number of trees, all the metrics
are increasing. Although the RF classifiers obtain very good results when it comes to
finding NSR rhythms, they perform quite poorly in finding AF rhythms. The highest
sensitivity and specificity are obtained with the 5 trees model and they are 42.05%
and 95.44%, respectively. Overall, this model also performs best with accuracy and
F1-score of 81.62% and 0.5421, respectively. The reason for the low F1-score is due
to the sensitivity and PPV (not shown in the table) being low. As the F1-score is the
harmonic mean of these two metrics, the F1-score will be low.
The results for the sensitivity show that increasing the number of trees, in
turn, decreases the sensitivity. Although the same thing happens to some degree for
the specificity, the difference between the results is smaller compared to the sensitivity
results. The same occurs for the accuracy and F1-score. Interestingly, the plateau
observed in the validation set results seems to be present in the test set results as
well. As can be noticed for the 30 trees model, 40 trees model and 50 trees model the
results across the metrics are the same.

In Table 5.14, it can be seen that, for the validation set, an increasing num-

the difference is only approximately 5.5 seconds. Therefore, this trade-off might not

The results for the test set, however, show quite different results from the
validation set results.
Here, an increasing number of trees does not equal higher
accuracy and F1-score, etc. On the contrary, the model with the least number of
trees (5 trees) performs best across all metrics except the specificity. The accuracy
and F1-score of this model are 84.15% and 0.6488, respectively. With 5 trees, the
specificity is 93.50% which is the lowest compared to the other number of trees. The
second-best performing model is the one with 20 trees. This model achieves accuracy
and F1-score of 83.91% and 0.6068, respectively and is therefore comparable to the
model with 5 trees.
For the RF classifiers in the test set, one thing is in common. The classifiers
can find NSR rhythms quite good. However, they do a poor job when it comes to
finding AF rhythms. Overall though, the results for the M = 20 beats segment are
better than those with M = 60 beats per segment (and M = 128 beats per segment).
This is contrary to the findings in [35], where it was found that increasing the segment
length resulted in higher accuracy, etc.

5.4.2.2
SVM With Different Kernels
This section covers the results of the SVM classifier used on the MITDB dataset.
Table 5.16 and Table 5.17 show the results of this classifier with different kernels and
with M = 60 beats per segment. The results are shown for the validation set and
test set, respectively.
For the validation set in Table 5.16, it can be seen that the highest accuracy
and F1-score is achieved with the quadratic kernel. The accuracy and F1-score with
this kernel is 99.93% and 0.9993, respectively. With this kernel type, an AUC of 1
has also been achieved, suggesting that it has been possible to perfectly separate the
two classes in the validation set. This also counts for the cubic kernel and the RBF
kernel. For the linear kernel, however, the result is very close to 1 (0.99932). The
quadratic kernel also has the highest specificity of 99.86%. It is noticeable that for
all the kernel types, the sensitivity is 100%, suggesting that with all the kernel types,
it has been possible to correctly classify all the AF instances (validation set).
When these results are compared to the ones in Table 5.8, it can be seen
that the quadratic kernel is the best performing kernel for both the MITDB dataset
and the AFDB dataset.
In both instances, the segment length is M = 60 beats
per segment. The shortest training time has been obtained with the linear kernel.

Although this provides the shortest training time, it still takes approximately double
the time to train compared to the slowest model with the RF classifier (40 trees, Table
5.12).

In the test set (Table 5.17), the results are not completely consistent with

has a lower accuracy (79.56%) compared to the quadratic and cubic kernels (81.47%),

accuracy of 82.94%. However, this kernel type also has the lowest F1-score. This
suggests that the sensitivity must be low for this kernel type. When inspecting Ta-

Table 5.18 and Table 5.19 show the results for the validation set and test
set, respectively, with the SVM classifier and M = 20 beats per segment.
The results for the validation set somehow agrees with the findings in the
validation set with M = 60 beats per segment. Here, the quadratic kernel also has
the highest accuracy and F1-score of 99.94% and 0.9994, respectively. However, for
this segment length, the cubic kernel achieves the same results with a slightly higher
specificity of 99.92% against 99.90% for the quadratic kernel. For all the kernels, the
AUC is pretty much 1, which suggests that they have been able to perfectly separate
the two classes in the validation set.
The results of the test set are listed in Table 5.19 and they are similar to the
ones of the validation set in some aspects. In the test set, the SVM classifier with a
linear kernel yet again outperforms the other kernels. With an accuracy and F1-score
of 90.09% and 0.7789, respectively, this kernel type achieves quite good results. The
linear kernel also achieves the highest sensitivity and the lowest specificity of 67.98%
and 97.22%, respectively. This is consistent with the findings for the test set with
M = 60 beats per segment. Here, the best model (linear kernel) had the highest
F1-score and sensitivity, but the lowest specificity among the kernel types. For the
SVM classifier in general, it seems to have the same problem as the RF classifer. Both
classifiers perform good when it comes to correctly finding NSR rhythms. The SVM
classifier seems to do this a bit better than the RF classifier with a higher specificity
in general for the test sets, especially for the shortest segment length.
However, the problem with both classifiers is that they do a poor job in
correctly classifying AF rhythms. The SVM classifier still perform a bit better than
the RF classifier when correctly classifying AF rhythms is of concern. This is also
seen in the slightly higher accuracy and F1-score for the SVM classifier. The trade-off
of the better performance is yet again the training time. For all the different segment
lengths, the fact is that SVM takes a longer time to train a model compared to RF
no matter which kernel type is used.
