classifier and especially the RF classifier. Compared to the SVM classifier, the kNN
classifier takes approximately double the time to train a model. Compared to the

train a model.

5.4.3
Training on AFDB and Testing on MITDB
This section deals with the results of the last experiment, where the trained models
from the AFDB dataset are tested on the whole MITDB dataset (all 48 subjects).
Because the non-AF rhythms of these two datasets are not equal, only AF features
and labels will be used as input for the trained models. As a result, only TPs and
FNs can be found on the confusion matrices. As no TNs and FPs are present, the
specificity cannot be calculated (division by zero), PPV will always be 100% and the
accuracy will be equal to the sensitivity. Therefore, the sensitivity and F1-score only
will be shown in the results. Furthermore, as no specificity can be calculated, ROC
curve plots cannot be made and the AUC cannot be calculated. As the PPV is always
100% and as the F1-score is the harmonic mean of sensitivity and PPV, the F1-score
shown in the next tables will be quite larger than the sensitivity. By giving only AF
features and labels as input, it is only possible to see how good the trained models are
at predicting AF rhythm. The ability of the trained models at finding other rhythms
is therefore unknown.
As with the previous sections, the results will be divided into three parts
according to the classifier used, starting with RF followed by SVM and kNN. Because
it was not possible to train SVM models and kNN models with M = 20 beats per
==PAGE:80==
60
5 Results and Discussion
segment and M = 128 beats per segment on the AFDB dataset, these results cannot
be shown.
5.4.3.1
RF With Different Number of Trees
Table 5.24, Table 5.25 and Table 5.26 show the results for the RF classifier with
M = 128, M = 60 and M = 20 beats per segment, respectively.
For M = 128 beats per segment (Table 5.24), it is evident that there is a
certain pattern when it comes to the number of trees and the sensitivity. For almost
all the trained RF classifiers (not with 30 trees), increasing the number of trees results
in an increased sensitivity and F1-score. Therefore, the model with 50 trees performs
best in terms of correctly finding AF rhythm. The sensitivity and F1-score of the
model with 50 trees is 85.50% and 0.9236, respectively. For the model with 5 trees
and 10 trees, the performance is very poor and it seems that for M = 128 beats per
segment, the number of trees should be increased.

quadratic kernel was good at correctly labeling rhythms during the training and

in Table 5.17, where the validation set and test set were from subjects of the MITDB

5.5 Limitations of the Model and Future Perspectives

5.5
Limitations of the Model and Future Perspectives
As with any other model, the proposed algorithm in this thesis is not perfect. This
has been shown in multiple instances. With the results shown in the last couple of
sections, it was seen that none of the models in the test set were able to distinguish
AF rhythms from NSR rhythms or other rhythms successfully. For two of the clas-
sifiers, SVM and RF, it was possible to correctly finding a lot of the NSR rhythms
(MITDB) and other rhythms (AFDB). However, correctly classifying AF rhythms
was only possible with the kNN classifer. This counts for both the MITDB dataset
and the AFDB dataset, but especially the MITDB dataset.
Ideally, the proposed algorithm should have both high sensitivity and high
specificity, meaning that it should be able to correctly label rhythms as AF, NSR and
other at the same time. The above findings are true for the experiments where the
training set and test set are from subjects from the same dataset. For the experiment
where the trained models from the AFDB dataset were tested on the MITDB dataset,
the results were a bit different. With only AF features and labels used as input for
the trained models, the RF and SVM classifiers were reasonably good at finding AF
rhythm. Although the kNN classifier performed decently in this experiment, it lacks
behind the two other classifiers.
There could be some reasons for these results. First, the trained models
might be badly overfitting.
The idea with the 10-fold cross-validation is that it
should prevent overfitting by assigning a portion of the dataset for training and an-
other portion of the dataset for validation. However, overfitting might still occur.
Overfitting is the instance where the trained models are very good at correctly label-
ing the points they have seen. When it comes to labeling new data, the performance
drops significantly compared to the training (and validation) set. This is exactly what
has happened for some of the experiments. For the longest segment, especially, there
is generally a big difference between the validation set results and the test set results.
==PAGE:86==
66
5 Results and Discussion
Another reason could be the zero-padding. As it was described in section
4.4 and section 4.4.1, zero-padding has been conducted 2 times. The first time was
before the tensor construction as each of the segments needed to be of same length
to construct a tensor for each subject in the dataset. The second time was before
extraction of the 5 statistical features. The reason for this was that to concatenate all
the tensors (for each subject) into one tensor along the third dimension, the second
dimension of the tensors must be consistent. To achieve this consistency, zeros were
padded. The issue with this zero padding, and especially the second time, is that they
influence the statistical features that were extracted. For instance, when considering
the final tensor before extraction of the 5 statistical features, Rn1×n2×n3, the content
of the variable in MATLAB for the MITDB dataset (first 5 subjects) with M = 128
beats per segment looks as follows:
R =


90 × 13012 × 34
90 × 16132 × 29
90 × 13246 × 33
90 × 14242 × 31
90 × 13016 × 39


(5.1)
Looking at equation (5.1), it is evident that the number of samples per
segment differs between the subjects. However, for the first 5 subjects, the difference
is not significant and the padded zeros might not have a large influence on the final
result. As the zeros are padded to match the longest segment, the tensor in equation
(5.1) will become:
R =


90 × 16132 × 34
90 × 16132 × 29
90 × 16132 × 33
90 × 16132 × 31
90 × 16132 × 39


(5.2)
After concatenating the tensors along the third dimension, the final stacked
tensor for the first 5 subjects in the MITDB dataset will be:
==PAGE:87==
5.5 Limitations of the Model and Future Perspectives
67
R =
[
90 × 16132 × 166
]
(5.3)
For the first 5 subjects, this might not be a big problem.
However, for
subject 231 in the MITDB dataset, the tensor before feature extraction is:
R =
[
90 × 26403 × 23
]
(5.4)
The problem with the tensor in equation (5.4) is that all the other tensors
will be zero-padded along the second dimension such that they will have 26403 sam-
ples in each segment. For some of the tensors, therefore, they will contain more zeros
than non-zeros along the second dimension. This will count for subject 100 (the first
subject), for instance. Therefore, when calculating the mean of the DWT-EMD de-
compositions, the result will be closer to zero as these zeros are padded.
When considering the train-test split (training+validation and testing) of
the MITDB dataset, subject 231 is included in the training and validation set. The
trained models will thus have seen the zeros that have been padded in terms of the
influenced statistical features. As the same number of zeros have not necessarily been
padded in the test set, the trained model will have a hard time correctly classifying
rhythms in the test set. This counts for both the MITDB dataset as well as the
AFDB dataset.
For the AFDB dataset, the problem is even larger. Recall that this dataset
was split into 6 groups, where the first 5 groups contained recordings from 4 subjects.
The last group contained recordings of 3 subjects. For the first group, the second di-
mension after zero-padding might have a length of 250.000 samples, for instance. This
number might be 350.000 for the second group, etc. The issue with this is that now
each group has different number of zeros padded along the second dimension. After
the zero-padding, the groups are stacked into one tensor and the 5 statistical features
are calculated and models were later trained. For these trained models, the input data
have been features where each subject tensor as well as each group has been subject
to different amount of zeros padded. This was not the issue with the MITDB dataset.
==PAGE:88==
68
5 Results and Discussion
One way to cope with the problem of zero-padding is to have fixed length
segments of the heartbeats. The idea with fixed length segments is that after the R
peaks have been found, each of the segments will start from X samples or seconds
before the R peak and end Y samples or seconds after the R peak. For instance, in
their article, Geirnaert et al. have conducted a fixed length segmentation where each
segment starts 250 ms before an R peak and ends 330 ms after an R peak [7]. The
reason for choice of these values is to make sure that both the P wave and the T
wave are included in each of the segmented heartbeats. After these heartbeats are
segmented, they can be stacked in a tensor, as shown in Figure 5.14. Here, it can be
seen that each segmented heartbeat is covered by each of the red frames (left plot).
A small fraction of the ECG signals is left out for each segment, but the contribution
of this fraction to the classification of AF rhythms might be negligible anyway. Each
of these segmented heartbeats are then stacked into a vector as seen in the plot on
the right side in Figure 5.14. For the proposed algorithm in this thesis, the DWT and
EMD decompositions would then be conducted on this tensor.
One problem with this method is that it would not be possible to explore
how good the model performs when it comes to different segment lengths. With the
model in this thesis, different number of heartbeats are included in each segment.This
will make it possible to determine how good the model is at detecting AF in shorter
ECG recordings as well as longer ECG recordings. When the ECG recordings are
segmented as in Figure 5.14, each segment will contain one heartbeat only.
Figure 5.14: Fixed length segments of heartbeats that are stacked in a tensor [26].
One drawback of the DWT-EMD decomposition is the computational time
==PAGE:89==
5.5 Limitations of the Model and Future Perspectives
69
and load. As stated in section 4.1.2, the subject of the AFDB dataset were divided
into 6 groups. Because the EMD algorithm is computationally heavy and because the
ECG recordings of the AFDB dataset are 10 hours in duration, the High Performance
Computers at DTU needed to be used. For the third group only and with M = 128
beats per segment, the memory requirement was set to 256 GB. After almost 5.5
hours, the DWT-EMD decomposition as well as the feature extraction completed
and the total memory used was a little above 218 GB. For M = 60 beats per seg-
ment, the computations for the same group took over 8 hours to complete. With a
normal modern computer, this computation would take a lot longer time to complete.
For the MITDB dataset, a computer with 32 GB memory and 2.80 GHz
CPU was used and the decompositions and feature extraction were completed. For
M = 128 beats per segment and M = 60 beats per segment, the decomposition and
feature extraction took approximately 25 minutes to complete. For M = 20 beats
per segment, the same things took approximately 45 minutes to complete. Although
these times include both the decompositions and the feature extraction, most of the
computational time was contributed by the DWT-EMD decomposition (especially the
EMD decomposition). The reason for the increased computational time for M = 20
beats per segment is the number of points. The result of the 50% overlap and the
shorter segments is a higher number of segments, thus a higher number of points to
be analyzed.
For the computational time of the DWT-EMD decomposition, it can there-
fore be concluded that this method is best suited for shorter ECG recordings. This is
further confirmed by the results presented in section 5.4.1 and section 5.4.2 (and their
subsections), which show that generally for shorter segments, the classifiers seem to
better classify the different rhythms.
Majority vote was applied to label segments as either NSR segment or AF
segment in the MITDB dataset and AF segment or other rhythm segments in the
AFDB dataset. The choice of majority vote is based on the findings in Dash et al.
[35], where they found that their model performed best when majority vote was ap-
plied to the segments. Besides majority vote, lower thresholds could be used in the
proposed algorithm in this thesis. For instance, thresholds of 10%, 20% and 30%
could be used. This would mean that, for instance, if 10% or more of the segment
contained AF rhythm, the whole segment would be labeled as an AF segment.
==PAGE:90==
70
5 Results and Discussion
The issue with the lower thresholds is that they mostly contain information
from the non-AF rhythms. For instance, for M = 20 beats per segment, assume that
one segment contains non-AF rhythm only and another segment contains 2 beats
with AF rhythm and the rest of the beats contain non-AF rhythm. With a threshold
of 10%, the first segment will be labeled non-AF segment and the second segment
will be labeled AF rhythm as 10% of the beats contain AF rhythm. However, when
looking at the features of the two segments, it is highly probable that they are very
similar. The reason for this is that most of the second segment contains non-AF
rhythm but it is still considered an AF segment because of the low threshold used.
The issue with the lower threshold is the possibility of a classifier not being able to
separate the two classes as their features will be quite similar. This would probably
be more difficult for the very low thresholds, such as the 10% threshold and to some
degree the 20% threshold as well.
The feature extraction after the DWT-EMD decomposition has been con-
ducted to — as the name states — extract features. Additionally, this feature extrac-
tion results in a dimensionality reduction, which in turn results in a reduced training
time for the different classifiers as fewer points are given as input. Instead of using
the 5 statistical features, autoencoders could be used.
Autoencoders are a type of artificial neural networks which are used to learn
data coding [54]. They are typically used for noise removal and dimensionality reduc-
tion. For the proposed algorithm in this thesis, the autoencoders could be utilized for
dimensionality reduction. Autoencoders consist of two main parts: An encoder part
and a decoder part. The encoder part deals with learning a representation of the data
by mapping the data into a code. The dimension of this code is less than the original
data. The decoder part, on the other hand, maps the code to a reconstruction of
the original input data. By using this code as a feature for the classifiers, two things
could be achieved:
1. The ”raw” data from the DWT-EMD decomposition is used as input to the
classifier.
2. The dimension is reduced as fewer points are used, which in turn will result in
a faster trained model.
==PAGE:91==
5.5 Limitations of the Model and Future Perspectives
71
The zero-padding would still be conducted even though autoencoders are used. How-
ever, their influence might be less significant.
==PAGE:92==
72
==PAGE:93==
CHAPTER 6
Conclusion
This study aimed to propose a model where multi-lead ECG recordings could be
used simultaneously to detect AF. ECG recordings were collected from the MITDB
database and the AFDB database. Since one of these databases were imbalanced —
the MITDB database — ADASYN was applied to balance the classes.
The ECG recordings from both databases contained noise, including baseline wander
where the baseline of the signals fluctuated. DWT was applied to remove this baseline
wander and it was removed successfully on both leads.
The segmentation of the signals was based on the location of the R peaks.
To detect these R peaks, the ecgpuwave function of the PhysioNet WFDB Toolbox
was used. Besides the detection of R peaks, this function can also detect the begin-
ning, end and peak location of the P wave and T wave.
Three segment lengths were used: M = {20, 60, 128} beats/R peaks per segment.
This means that each segment contained 20, 60 or 128 R peaks. By using these seg-
ment lengths, it was possible to see how the algorithm could perform in shorter and
longer recordings.
The segmentation of the signals was used to construct a third-order tensor for each
subject in the dataset. The first dimension of this tensor represented the number of
segments, the second dimension represented the number of samples in each segment
and the third dimension represented the number of leads.
Based on the tensors, DWT and EMD decompositions were conducted. First
the tensors were decomposed into nine subtensors using DWT with a total of nine
levels. Each of these subtensors were further decomposed using EMD with five IMFs.
This was conducted for both leads. The resulting tensors were rearranged such that
the first dimension represented the DWT-EMD decompositions, the second dimension
represented the number of samples in each segment and the last dimension contained
==PAGE:94==
74
6 Conclusion
the number of segments. With 9 subtensors from the DWT, 5 IMFs and 2 leads,
the first dimension was therefore 90. Based on these 90, five statistical features were
extracted: mean, standard deviation, variance, skewness and kurtosis yielding 450
features. Additionally, six HRV features were extracted which resulted in a total of
456 features.
For both databases, a train/test split was conducted where approximately
80% of the data was assigned to training and the rest to testing. The reason for this
was the annotation of the databases. Both databases contained AF rhythm annota-
tions but the annotations of the second class differed between the databases.
In addition, the trained models from the AFDB database were tested on the MITDB
database, where only AF features and labels were used as input for the trained models.
It was found that the algorithm performs best for the shortest segment
length for both databases. For the AFDB database, the RF classifier with 5 trees
performed best in the test set with an accuracy, sensitivity and F1-score of 78.37%,
82.86% and 0.7792, respectively. For the MITDB database, an SVM classifier with
the linear kernel performed best in the test set with an accuracy, sensitivity and F1-
score of 90.09%, 67.98% and 0.7789, respectively. For this model, the specificity was
97.22%.
When the trained models from the AFDB database were tested on the
MITDB database, the algorithm again performed best for the shortest segment length.
Here, the RF classifier with 5 trees was again the best performing model with a sen-
sitivity and F1-score of 92.05% and 0.9586, respectively.
Things could be done differently in the proposed algorithm.
First, fixed
length segments could be used to cope with the issue that might exist with the zero-
padding. As the segment lengths will be fixed this way, no zeros-padding would be
needed as all segments have the same length. Additionally, it could be interesting to
use autoencoders instead of calculation of the statistical features. With autoencoders
it would be possible to have a representation of the DWT-EMD decompositions as
well as dimensionality reduction.

1 %% Load ECG signals
2 clear,clc
3
4 % Use '%05.f' to add leading zeros to number which is transformed to
5 % string by num2str.
6
7 Data1 = [04015, 04043, 04048, 04126, 04746, 04908, 04936, 05091, 05121,
05261 ...
8
06426, 06453, 06995, 07162, 07859, 07879];
9
10
11 for i = 1:length(Data1)
12
[sig_1{i}, Fs1, ~] = rdsamp(num2str(Data1(i),'%05.f'),1); % Lead I
13
[sig_2{i}, Fs2, ~] = rdsamp(num2str(Data1(i),'%05.f'),2); % Lead II
14 end
15
16 %% Denoise signal
17
18 y1 = dwt_denoise1(sig_1,8,Data1); % Denoising Lead I
19 y2 = dwt_denoise2(sig_2,8,Data1); % Denoising Lead II
20
21 %% Resample
22
23 [p,q] = rat(125/Fs1);
24
25 for i = 1:length(Data1)
26
y1_new{i} = resample(y1{i}, p, q);
27
y2_new{i} = resample(y2{i}, p, q);
28 end
29 clear p q
30

32

34
[ann2{i}, anntype2{i}, ~, ~, ~, comments{i}] = rdann(num2str(Data1(i),'
%05.f'),'atr');
35 end

B MATLAB Code

38
39 for i = 1:length(Data1)

==PAGE:110==
90
B MATLAB Code
221 toc
222 %% Reshape EMDs
223 tic
224 % Reshape all the perm matrices
225 for i = 1:length(perm)
226
resh{1,i} = reshape(perm{1,i},[],size(perm{1,i},5),size(perm{1,i},4));
227 end
228 toc
229 %% Zero padding the tensor to max length
230 tic
231 [~,b,~] = cellfun(@size, resh);
232
233 idx_pad = max(b) - b;
234
235 for index = 1:length(resh)
236
index
237
resh_padded{1,index} = padarray(resh{1,index}, [0 idx_pad(index) 0],0,'
post');
238 end

240
241 %% Concatenate zeropadded 3D arrays along 3rd axis
242 clear resh
243 stacked = cat(3,resh_padded{:});
244 clear resh_padded
245
246 %% Calculate statistical features of DWT+EMD
247
248 % Because of the large memory requirements , this part of the algorithm is
249 % computed using the DTU High Performance Computers.
250 tic
251 mu = squeeze(double(mean(stacked ,2))); % mean
252 st = squeeze(double(std(stacked ,0,2))); % standard deviation
253 v = squeeze(double(var(stacked ,0,2))); % variance
254
255 skew = nan(90,1,size(stacked ,3)); % skewness
256 kurt = nan(90,1,size(stacked ,3)); % kurtosis
257
258 for i = 1:size(skew,1)
259
skew(i,:,:) = double(skewness(stacked(i,:,:),1,2));
260
kurt(i,:,:) = double(kurtosis(stacked(i,:,:),1,2));
261 end
262 toc
263
264 skew = squeeze(skew);
265 kurt = squeeze(kurt);
266
267 feat_cat = cat(1,mu,st,v,skew,kurt);

Code/TensorConstruct.m
1 function [out_featuresSyn , out_labelsSyn] = ADASYN(in_features , in_labels,
in_beta, in_kDensity , in_kSMOTE, in_featuresAreNormalized)
2 %this function implements the ADASYN method as proposed in the following
3 %paper:
4 %
5 %[1]: H. He, Y. Bai, E.A. Garcia, and S. Li, "ADASYN: Adaptive Synthetic
6 %Sampling Approach for Imbalanced Learning", Proc. Int'l. J. Conf. Neural
7 %Networks, pp. 1322--1328, (2008).
8 %
9 %the implementation follows the notation and equation numbers given in
10 %section 3.1.4 of another paper:
11 %
12 %[2]: H. He and E.A. Garcia, "Learning from imbalanced data",
13 %Knowledge and Data Engineering , IEEE Transactions on 21, no. 9,
14 %pp. 1263--1284, (2009).
15 %
16 %
17 %the purpose of the ADASYN method is to improve class balance towards
18 %equally-sized classes for a given input dataset. this is achieved by
19 %synthetically creating new examples from the minority class via linear
20 %interpolation between existing minority class samples. this approach is
21 %known as the SMOTE method, cf. section 3.1.3 in [2]. ADASYN is an
22 %extension of SMOTE, creating more examples in the vicinity of the boundary
23 %between the two classes, than in the interior of the minority class.
24 %cf. the supplied script demo_ADASYN for an example of this.
25 %
26 %
27 % INPUTS:
28 %----------
29 %in_features:
30 %(N \times P) matrix of numerical features. each row is one example, each
31 %column is one feature, hence there are N examples with P features each.
32 %
33 %in_labels:
34 %boolean N-vector of labels, defining the classes to which the examples in
35 %in_features belong.
36 %
==PAGE:159==
B MATLAB Code
139
37 %in_beta [default: 1]:
38 %desired level of balance, where 0 means that the size of the minority
39 %class will not be changed, and 1 means that the minority class will be
40 %ADASYNed to have (approximately , due to rounding) the same size as the
41 %majority class. any value of in_beta between 0 and 1 provides a compromise
42 %between these two extremes.
43 %note that in_beta IS NOT the resulting class ratio, but a percentage of
44 %how much class balance is improved in comparison to the given class
45 %balance! 0 means nothing is improved in comparison to the given class
46 %balance and 1 means class sizes are perfectly equalized (except for small
47 %rounding-related deviations).
48 %
49 %in_kDensity [default: 5]:
50 %k for kNN used in ADASYN density estimation, i.e. in calculation of the
51 %\Gamma_i values in eq. (4) of reference [2]. this is the kNN call that
52 %regards examples from both classes.
53 %
54 %in_kSMOTE [default: 5]:
55 %k for kNN used in subsequent SMOTE-style synthesis of new examples.
56 %this is the kNN call that regards only examples from the minority class.
57 %cf. eq. (1) in reference [2].
58 %
59 %in_featuresAreNormalized [default: true]:
60 %boolean indicating whether the features (i.e. the different columns) in
61 %in_features are already normalized to the same scale or not.
62 %by default normalized features are assumed as the input, i.e. the user is
63 %expected to apply a normalization method of choice before passing the data
64 %to the ADASYN function.
65 %the practical difference in the two values of in_featuresAreNormalized is
66 %the following:
67 %true:
Euclidean distance is used in all kNN calls. for reasonable
68 %
results, in_features should already be normalized when calling
69 %
ADASYN().
70 %false: standardized Euclidean distance (type "doc knnsearch" into MATLAB
71 %
console and look for 'seuclidean' for an explanation) is used in
72 %
all kNN calls. any normalization already present in in_features is
73 %
ignored, and instead unit variance normalization is used.
74 %
however, this does NOT modify the data. the normalization is only
75 %
applied within knnsearch.
76 %
77 %
78 % OUTPUTS:
79 %----------
80 %out_featuresSyn , out_labelsSyn:
81 %features and labels of ONLY the synthetically created examples.
82 %note that each entry of out_labelsSyn is the label of the minority class
83 %since only examples of the minority class are created.
84 %concatenating [in_features out_featuresSyn] and [in_labels out_labelsSyn]
==PAGE:160==
140
B MATLAB Code
85 %gives a new example set with the desired class balance.
86 %
87 %
88 %
89 %
90 %-------------------------------------------------------------------------
91 % Version: 1.0
92 % Date: 2015-04-17
93 % Author: Dominic Siedhoff
94 %-------------------------------------------------------------------------
95 %
96 %
97 %-------------------------------------------------------------------------
98 % Copyright (c) 2015 Dominic Siedhoff
99 %-------------------------------------------------------------------------
100 %
101 % License: This software may be freely used, shared and modified. It must
102 %
not be sold. It is provided without any explicit or implicit
103 %
warranty of any kind. This license text must be included with
104 %
every copy made.
105 %
106 %-------------------------------------------------------------------------
107
108
109
110
111 if nargin < 3 || isempty(in_beta)
112
in_beta = 1;
113 end
114
115 if nargin < 4 || isempty(in_kDensity)
116
in_kDensity = 5;
117 end
118
119 if nargin < 5 || isempty(in_kSMOTE)
120
in_kSMOTE = 5;
121 end
122
123 if nargin < 6 || isempty(in_featuresAreNormalized)
124
in_featuresAreNormalized = true;
125 end
126
127
128 if in_beta == 0
129
%nothing needs to be done because beta==0 is defined to mean that
130
%current class ratio is kept:
131
out_featuresSyn = [];
132
out_labelsSyn
= [];
==PAGE:161==
B MATLAB Code
141
133
return;
134 end
135
136
137 if ~all(in_labels==0 | in_labels==1)
138
error('ADASYN: in_labels may contain only the values 0 and 1.');
139 end
140
141
142 numZeros = sum(in_labels==0);
143 numOnes
= sum(in_labels==1);
144
145 if numOnes == numZeros
146
%nothing needs to be done because if classes are already balanced, then
147
%for any in_beta, this is already the desired result.
148
out_featuresSyn = [];
149
out_labelsSyn
= [];
150
return;
151 else
152
if numZeros > numOnes
153
majLabel = logical(0);
154
minLabel = logical(1);
155
else
156
majLabel = logical(1);
157
minLabel = logical(0);
158
end
159 end
160
161 %rename:
162 S = in_features;
163 clear in_features;
164
165 %feature sets by class:
166 Smin = S(in_labels==minLabel ,:);
167 Smaj = S(in_labels==majLabel ,:);
168
169 %eq (3):
170 G = (size(Smaj,1) - size(Smin,1)) * in_beta;
171 G = round(G);
172
173 %handle boundary cases:
174 if size(Smin,1)==0
175
warning('ADASYN: there were no examples of the minority class in the
data. hence balancing is not possible. Returning empty matrices.');
176
out_featuresSyn = [];
177
out_labelsSyn
= [];
178
return;
179 end
==PAGE:162==
142
B MATLAB Code
180
181 if size(Smin,1)==1
182
warning('ADASYN: there was only one example of the minority class in the
data. Hence returning G copies of that single example for balancing
.');
183
out_featuresSyn = repmat(Smin, [G 1]);
184
out_labelsSyn
= logical(minLabel * ones([G 1]));
185
return;
186 end
187
188
189 if in_featuresAreNormalized
190
knnDistance = 'euclidean';
191 else
192
%IMPORTANT: using 'seuclidean' as the distance measure means that
193
%standardized Euclidean distance is used, i.e. the standard deviation
194
%of the coordinates is automatically divided away. hence, using
195
%'seuclidean' instead of 'euclidean' saves the effort of normalizing
196
%the feature values by a Z-transformation (0mean,1var).
197
%cf. documentation of input parameter in_featuresAreNormalized for more
198
%information.
199
knnDistance = 'seuclidean';
200 end
201
202 %kNN for density estimation:
203 idcs = knnsearch_nonflat(S,Smin, 'K',in_kDensity+1, 'Distance',knnDistance);
204 %note: why in_kDensity+1? because Smin is a subset of S and hence all
205 %points in Smin have a trivial nearest neighbor in S with distance 0.
206 %but that neighbor is not interesting because it's the point from Smin
207 %itself. hence remove it:
208 idcs = idcs(:,2:end);
209
210
211 %compute the \Gamma values (eq. (4) in reference [2]):
212 Gamma = zeros([size(Smin,1) 1]);
213 for cmi=1:size(Smin,1)
%cmi: current minority example index
214
cNNs = idcs(cmi,:);
%current NearestNeighbors
215
cNNsLabels = in_labels(cNNs);
%labels of cNNs:
216
cNNsLabelsMaj = (cNNsLabels == majLabel);
217
cDelta = nnz(cNNsLabelsMaj);
%the Delta_i of eq. (4) in reference [2]
218
219
%write Gamma, not yet normalized:
220
Gamma(cmi) = cDelta / in_kDensity;
221
222 end
223
224 %normalize Gamma to give a distribution function:
225 if sum(Gamma)==0
==PAGE:163==
B MATLAB Code
143
226
%if there is no class overlap w.r.t. these knn settings, create a
227
%uniform distribution:
228
Gamma = 1/length(Gamma) * ones(size(Gamma));
229 else
230
%create nonuniform distribution:
231
Gamma = Gamma / sum(Gamma);
%this makes it exactly eq. (4) in
reference [2]
232 end
233
234 %compute g_i (eq. (5) in reference [2]):
235 %these g_i are the numbers of synthetic examples to be generated from each
236 %example in Smin
237 g = round(Gamma * G);
238
239 if sum(g)==0
240
warning('ADASYN: Classes are already well-balanced (i.e. sum(g)==0).
Returning empty matrices.');
241
out_featuresSyn = [];
242
out_labelsSyn
= [];
243
return;
244 end
245
246 %with this g known, call the ADASYN_SMOTE subroutine...:
247 out_featuresSyn = ADASYN_SMOTE(Smin,g,in_kSMOTE,knnDistance);
248 %...and generate the labels:
249 out_labelsSyn = logical(minLabel * ones([size(out_featuresSyn ,1) 1]));
250
251
252
253 function Ssyn = ADASYN_SMOTE(Smin,g,k,knnDistance)
254 %subroutine implementing SMOTE algorithm as it is to be used by function
255 %ADASYN(). cf. section 3.1.3 in the following paper for details:
256 %
257 %[2]: H. He and E.A. Garcia, "Learning from imbalanced data",
258 %Knowledge and Data Engineering , IEEE Transactions on 21, no. 9,
259 %pp. 1263--1284, (2009).
260 %
261 %
262 % INPUTS:
263 %----------
264 %
265 %Smin:
266 %minority set from ADASYN()
267 %
268 %g:
269 %minority example synthesis counts as computed by ADASYN()
270 %
271 %k:
==PAGE:164==
144
B MATLAB Code
272 %number of neighbors to be regarded in kNN for SMOTE algorithm
273 %
274 %knnDistance:
275 %distance function used in kNN for SMOTE algorithm. depends on ADASYN's
276 %parameter in_featuresAreNormalized. please type "help ADASYN" into
277 %MATLAB's console for more information
278 %
279 %
280 % OUTPUTS:
281 %----------
282 %Ssyn: set of synthetic examples created from input set Smin by applying
283 %the SMOTE algorithm
284
285
286 %determine nearest neighbors:
287 idcs = knnsearch_nonflat(Smin,Smin, 'K',k+1, 'Distance',knnDistance);
288 %note: why k+1? because we search kNNs of Smin in Smin itself and hence all
289 %points in Smin have a trivial nearest neighbor in Smin with distance 0.
290 %but that neighbor is not interesting because it's the point from Smin
291 %itself. hence remove it:
292 idcs = idcs(:,2:end);
293
294 %initialize output and writing target as an empty matrix
295 Ssyn = zeros([0 size(Smin,2)]);
296
297 %for every minority example xi...
298 for cei=1:size(Smin,1)
%cei: current example index
299
300
%current minority example:
301
xi = Smin(cei,:);
302
303
%number of synthetic examples to be created from xi:
304
gi = g(cei);
305
306
%allocate space for gi examples to be created from xi:
307
xiSyn = zeros(gi, size(Smin,2));
308
309
%...iterate over synthetic examples to be created from xi and
310
%random partner from set of nearest neighbors:
311
for csi=1:gi
%csi: current synthetic example index
312
313
%get random partner example from nearest neighbors of xi:
314
%neighbor index:
315
nIdx = idcs(cei, randi(size(idcs,2)));
316
%neighbor:
317
xiHat = Smin(nIdx,:);
318
319
%create synthetic example as according to eq. (1) in reference [2]:
==PAGE:165==
B MATLAB Code
145
320
delta = rand(1);
321
xSyn = xi + delta * (xiHat - xi);
322
323
%write it to xiSyn:
324
xiSyn(csi,:) = xSyn;
325
326
end
327
328
%append examples synthesized from xi to overall synthetic example set:
329
Ssyn = [Ssyn; xiSyn];
330
331 end
332
333

336
337 function [IDX,D] = knnsearch_nonflat(X,Y, varargin)
338 %wraps knnsearch from MATLAB's statistics toolbox.
339 %knnsearch_nonflat executes knnsearch only on the dimensions with nonzero
340 %standard deviation, i.e. the flat dimensions are not passed on to
341 %knnsearch. this prevents pdist2 from producing the following warning in
342 %the context of standardized Euclidean distance ('seuclidean') in the
343 %presence of flat dimensions:
344 %"Warning: Some columns of S are zeros."
345 %if this warning occurs, pdist2 (and as a consequence knnsearch) gives only
346 %bad dummy results because the standardized Euclidean distance can not be
347 %computed properly in the presence of flat dimensions.
348 %using knnsearch_nonflat prevents this by filtering out flat dimensions.
349
350 nonflatX = std(X) ~= 0;
351 nonflatY = std(Y) ~= 0;
352
353 nonflat = nonflatX & nonflatY;
354
355 [IDX,D] = knnsearch(X(:,nonflat), Y(:,nonflat), varargin{:});
